  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_e1f4862b-67ee-456a-843a-751b2d03111b'
      });
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer9413918494698532666">Hard Times is the name of a town in the barren hills of the Dakota Territory. To this town there comes one day one of the reckless sociopaths who wander the West to kill and rape and pillage. By the time he is through and has ridden off, Hard Times is a smoking ruin. The de facto mayor, Blue, takes in two survivors of the carnage–a boy, Jimmy, and a prostitute, Molly, who </span>
  <span id="freeText9413918494698532666" style="display:none">Hard Times is the name of a town in the barren hills of the Dakota Territory. To this town there comes one day one of the reckless sociopaths who wander the West to kill and rape and pillage. By the time he is through and has ridden off, Hard Times is a smoking ruin. The de facto mayor, Blue, takes in two survivors of the carnage–a boy, Jimmy, and a prostitute, Molly, who has suffered unspeakably–and makes them his provisional family. Blue begins to rebuild Hard Times, welcoming new settlers, while Molly waits with vengeance in her heart for the return of the outlaw. Here is E. L. Doctorow’s debut novel, a searing allegory of frontier life that sets the stage for his subsequent classics.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9413918494698532666" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_223350736" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/223350736" />
    <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten"><img alt="Jeffrey Keeten" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1301087352p2/3427339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/223350736?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeffrey Keeten" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten">Jeffrey Keeten</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=the-old-west">the-old-west</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer223350736" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1349054141424624458"><b>“One day you stepped in snow, the next in mud, water soaked in your boots and froze them at night, it was the next worst thing to pure blizzardry, it was weather that wouldn't let you settle.”</b> <br/><br/>I read several Doctorows back in the late 1980s and never really clicked with him. His writing was fine; it just didn't blow my skirt up. For years though I have thought about picking up a copy of his first book Welcome to Hard Times. I usually like first books and I'm always intrigued with westerns that h</span>
  <span id="freeText1349054141424624458" style="display:none"><b>“One day you stepped in snow, the next in mud, water soaked in your boots and froze them at night, it was the next worst thing to pure blizzardry, it was weather that wouldn't let you settle.”</b> <br><br>I read several Doctorows back in the late 1980s and never really clicked with him. His writing was fine; it just didn't blow my skirt up. For years though I have thought about picking up a copy of his first book Welcome to Hard Times. I usually like first books and I'm always intrigued with westerns that have been written by people that usually don't write westerns. <br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/DoctorowBackCover_zps4a60ba22.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536179415i/26257624._SY540_.jpg" alt=" photo DoctorowBackCover_zps4a60ba22.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>E. L. Doctorow</b> <br><br><b>
  <i>Welcome to Hard Times</i>
</b> starts off with a bang. <i>"The Man from Bodie drank down a half bottle of the Silver Sun's best; that cleared the dust from his throat and then when Florence, who was a redhead, moved along the bar to him, he turned and grinned down at her. I guess Florence had never seen a man so big. Before she could say a word, he reached out and stuck his hand in the collar of her dress and ripped it down to her waist so that her breasts bounded out bare under the yellow light. We all scraped our chairs and stood up--none of us had ever looked at Florence that way before, for all she was. The saloon was full because we watched the man coming for a long time before he pulled in, but there was no sound now."</i><br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/WelcomeToHardTimes_zps28cd8348.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536179415i/26257625._SY540_.jpg" alt=" photo WelcomeToHardTimes_zps28cd8348.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>The movie was released in 1967 starring Henry Fonda.</b><br><br>Edmund Burke once said <b>"All that is necessary for the triumph of evil is that good men do nothing".</b> The men of Hard Times each reluctant to react, worried more about their personal safety than the security of the whole community failed to act. One man goes into the saloon with a piece of board intent on freeing Florence from the Man from Bodie. He staggers back out of the saloon, his head shattered and dies in the street. If other men had summoned the courage to go into that saloon with the one brave soul certainly things would have been different. I was reminded of one of my favorite movies High Noon. Hard Times was badly in need of a Gary Cooper character, but unfortunately the closest they had was a man named Blue. Blue was the pseudo mayor of Hard Times. He kept ledgers that tracked all the property and names of the individuals of the town. He settled squabbles. He was the only man who possibly could have rallied the men of the town and thrown the Man from Bodie from their midst. His highly developed sense of self preservation kept him from doing what had to be done. <br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/GaryCooper_zps9e12d0e3.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536179415i/26257626._SY540_.jpg" alt=" photo GaryCooper_zps9e12d0e3.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>Not every town has a Will Kane (Gary Cooper in High Noon).</b> <br><br>The town burned. Women were raped. People were murdered while others huddled in uncertain safety unwilling to lend a hand to those in direct need of assistance. The Man from Bodie leaves in the swirling smoke of his own destruction. <br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/BurningBuilding_zps0e577e56.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536179415i/26257627.jpg" alt=" photo BurningBuilding_zps0e577e56.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Blue left with a handful of survivors is determined to rebuild the town. Blue digs down and builds a dugout using sod for walls. I have pictures of the sod homes that my ancestors made when they first came to Kansas. There was no wood just prairie and sod was the only building material available. The Dakota territory where Hard Times is located proved as bereft of lumber as the flat lands of Kansas. <br><br>Blue recruits people to town, a tent city grows up then expands into real buildings as lumber is hauled up from the railroad. He is intent on rebuilding the town for reasons beyond his own understanding. They hire a sheriff. Things are going well until the mine that provided most of the revenue for the town plays out. Quickly on the heels of that disaster The Man From Bodie shows up again. He picks up right where he left off. The sheriff attempts to intervene and is shot and killed by The Man From Bodie. This time due to the economic uncertainly the out of work miners lend a helping hand to dismantling the town. <br><br>Blue is more proactive, even comes up with a plan that takes down The Man From Brodie, but can do nothing to save his town from the rampaging miners. <br><br>There are many subplots that explores human behavior that I haven't mentioned. This book is about average people who fail to organize and stand as one against the embodiment of evil (The Man From Bodie) and never recover from the devastating results of their failure to act.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1349054141424624458" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Howard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32041201-howard"><img alt="Howard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587918150p2/32041201.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2335105207?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Howard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Howard" href="/user/show/32041201-howard">Howard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780812978223" href="/book/show/1740510.Welcome_to_Hard_Times">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=dakota">dakota</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=american-west">american-west</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=film">film</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2335105207" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16145371770676575849">4.5 *<br/><br/><b>THE MOVIE</b><br/>I first learned that E.L. Doctorow had written a Western novel when I saw the film adaptation on television many years ago.<br/> <br/>I always read the movie credits (doesn’t everybody?) and back then they were much easier to read. That was because there were fewer of them (no gaffers, grips, or best boys), the lettering was much larger, and they were not scrolled.<br/><br/>So, when I read the credits for <i>Welcome to Hard Times</i>, I saw that it was based on a novel by E.L. Doctorow, which was published i</span>
  <span id="freeText16145371770676575849" style="display:none">4.5 *<br /><br /><b>THE MOVIE</b><br />I first learned that E.L. Doctorow had written a Western novel when I saw the film adaptation on television many years ago.<br /> <br />I always read the movie credits (doesn’t everybody?) and back then they were much easier to read. That was because there were fewer of them (no gaffers, grips, or best boys), the lettering was much larger, and they were not scrolled.<br /><br />So, when I read the credits for <i>Welcome to Hard Times</i>, I saw that it was based on a novel by E.L. Doctorow, which was published in 1960. Although the film was released in 1967, it had to be after 1975 that I saw it, because until the publication of <i>Ragtime</i> that year I had never heard of Doctorow, even though I later learned that it was his fourth novel.<br /><br />To be honest, despite a stellar cast headed by Henry Fonda, I didn’t think much of the movie. Maybe the problem was due to something else I recently learned. The film was originally planned as a TV movie. However, because of some rather graphic violence it was instead released as a theatrical film. Since I viewed the film on commercial TV (cable-TV arrived rather late in our area), the version I saw had to have had whole scenes edited from it.<br /><br />But the problem could have been Doctorow’s original story; he would not have been the first easterner who didn’t understand the historical West. However, my interest was piqued by the fact that I knew that Doctorow never wrote another Western and, even more than that, I later learned that it had been his debut novel. I found it ironic that this easterner (born in The Bronx and died in New York), a philosophy major no less, had begun his career as a novelist with a story set in the Dakota Territory.<br /> <br />Ever since seeing the film I have been on the lookout for a copy of the book. Oh, I could have ordered a copy from Amazon, but being <s>stingy</s> <s>tight</s> frugal, I didn’t want to pay ten dollars or more for a paperback when I anticipated that I wouldn’t even like the book. Then just a couple of weeks ago I found a copy at the local used bookstore for only two dollars. It had taken only forty years or so to locate one, and since I happened to have a couple of dollars on me, I bought it.<br /><br /><b>THE BOOK</b><br />Let me say right off the bat, the book is much better than the movie. Not only was a good cast wasted in the movie, so was a good story. They and Doctorow deserved better.<br /><br />Beginning with the very first paragraph in which we meet the Man from Bodie, it is apparent that we aren’t in Zane Grey territory. The second paragraph takes things a step farther and we begin to realize that this is one of the vilest villains to be found in a Western novel – and I have read a lot of them. In fact, he may have no peer.<br /><br />Unfortunately, there is no Gary Cooper type to make a stand at high noon and bring the bad man to heel. One man, Blue, the de facto mayor of the town, demonstrates more courage in dealing with the brute than he is given credit for, but being severely overmatched there isn’t much that he can do.<br /><br />After the Man from Bodie kills six people (I think; I may have overlooked someone), he burns the town down. When he finishes the town is more cemetery than town <br /><br />These events occur in the opening pages and therefore are not spoilers. They are the first things that the reader learns about the hardscrabble town of Hard Times, Dakota Territory, and what happened to it on that day.<br /><br />After the Man from Bodie departs, most of the town’s inhabitants do the same. With the destruction of the town there is nothing to keep them and the fear of the killer’s return is a good reason to skedaddle.<br /><br />Blue, on the other hand, does not leave. Instead he begins a campaign of encouraging people passing through to settle down and to help him rebuild the town. And some rather diverse characters do. Hanging over the town, however, at least among the original settlers, is the fear that the Man might return to the scene of his crimes, to rape, murder, and burn once more. <br /><br />This is not a formulaic or what could be called a genre Western, the kind that down through the decades has been responsible for more published books than any other genre. Instead of a Western, it could be classified as an anti-Western. It is a book that individuals who normally shy away from Westerns might want to read.<br /><br />It has hardly any stock Western characters. The main character is not a hero and, in fact, there are no heroes in the story, at least not in the traditional sense. Another important character is a prostitute, but she doesn’t have the requisite heart of gold, but one more like stone. And a boy who is orphaned when the Man From Bodie killed his father turns against his father figure who has taken him in.<br /><br />It is a grim allegorical tale that pits good against evil with the odds being heavily in favor of evil. Such a conflict has been written about countless times before, but Doctorow put a new twist on it. <br /><br />_________________<br /><br /><b>“… Doctorow cleverly turns the horse opera stereotype on its head. If you are not a fan of sagebrush prose, don’t let the notion that this is a “Western” dissuade you from reading this short, intense book. This is closer to Joseph Conrad than it is Louis L’Amour.”</b> – David Abrams, author of <i>Fobbit</i> and <i>Brave Deeds</i> (<i>The Quivering Pen</i> blog)</span>
  <a data-text-id="16145371770676575849" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16222970610095958362">"I told Molly we'd be ready for the bad man, but we can never be ready. Nothing is ever buried, the earth rolls in it's tracks, it never goes anywhere, it never changes, only the hope changes like morning and night, only the expectations rise and set. Why does there have to be promise before destruction?"<br/><br/>The Bad Man from Bodie is the villain in this book, but in reality he is just an embodiment for the evil that lies in men's hearts, for human nature gone terribly wrong.   <span id="freeText16222970610095958362" style="display:none">"I told Molly we'd be ready for the bad man, but we can never be ready. Nothing is ever buried, the earth rolls in it's tracks, it never goes anywhere, it never changes, only the hope changes like morning and night, only the expectations rise and set. Why does there have to be promise before destruction?"<br /><br />The Bad Man from Bodie is the villain in this book, but in reality he is just an embodiment for the evil that lies in men's hearts, for human nature gone terribly wrong. And the good people either ran in fear or just sat by and did nothing. Does that sound familiar? This book made my blood run cold.<br /><br />This book was first published in 1960, and was E. L. Doctorow's first novel. There is no way he could have intended it as a warning about the things happening in our present day world, so maybe it's just me choosing to interpret it that way.<br /><br />An incredible book any way you choose to look at it. You can read it as a western novel about the settling of a town, or as an allegory on the battle between good and evil. The last sentence blew me away.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1085772335106398065">GR friend Diane's quote when first starting this book, "The action starts on page 1 and never lets up." The first chapter reads a little like a comedy but that is short lived beginning with chapter 2. If you like fast-paced and action filled this would be a great book to take on. If you are looking for a happy book, keep in mind the title of this novel. Doctorow lays it all out for the reader to experience. This is a book <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/784866.Donald_Ray_Pollock' title='Donald Ray Pollock' rel='nofollow noopener'>Donald Ray Pollock</a> revealed has influenced/shaped his work as a writer.</span>

<div id="review_390385078" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/390385078" />
    <a title="Toby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5454475-toby"><img alt="Toby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542247337p2/5454475.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/390385078?book_show_action=true">Aug 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Toby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Toby" href="/user/show/5454475-toby">Toby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330250856" href="/book/show/1474894.Welcome_To_Hard_Times">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5454475-toby?shelf=western">western</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5454475-toby?shelf=lit">lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5454475-toby?shelf=black-as-night">black-as-night</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer390385078" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer847942503534939180"><b>I wanted a western in a noir style and I got it in spades.</b><br/><br/>Doctorow achieved in 155 pages what others spend 600 attempting; he has written a piece of literature in a popular style as an allegory for human nature whilst at the same time analysing what happened in Europe and specifically Germany in the 1930s, all the while making it an entertaining read. And this was his first novel!<br/><br/>Whilst this new title evokes a certain mood before you've even turned a page I think the original title of The Bad Ma</span>
  <span id="freeText847942503534939180" style="display:none"><b>I wanted a western in a noir style and I got it in spades.</b><br /><br />Doctorow achieved in 155 pages what others spend 600 attempting; he has written a piece of literature in a popular style as an allegory for human nature whilst at the same time analysing what happened in Europe and specifically Germany in the 1930s, all the while making it an entertaining read. And this was his first novel!<br /><br />Whilst this new title evokes a certain mood before you've even turned a page I think the original title of The Bad Man From Bodie has much greater effect after you reach then end. This Bad Man is the embodiment of all human evil and weakness, and in the face of such evil people have a habit of doing nothing. As far as revisionist westerns go this has to take pride of place in its rejection of the John Wayne ideal. There's a story that Clint Eastwood once wrote to John Wayne suggesting they work together on a western and in response John Wayne let Eastwood know in no uncertain terms that what he was doing to the genre was a disgrace. If that is true I'd hate to think what Wayne would think of the way Doctorow paints his western heroes in that case. These people are all weak, they are all cowards, and the novel is better for it.<br /><br />The cover of my Pan edition calls it terse and bloodthirsty, the narration feels like the darker, more existential brother of Billy Wilders Double Indemnity, Norman Mailer called it <i>'a superb novel'</i> whilst the New York Times compared it to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4900.Heart_of_Darkness_" title="Heart of Darkness  by Joseph Conrad" rel="nofollow noopener">Heart of Darkness</a> and called it <i>'taut and dramatic, exciting and successfully symbolic'</i>, I cannot believe this book is not more widely read in the 21st century and I implore you all to read it now.</span>
  <a data-text-id="847942503534939180" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6311021826347759636">This short first novel by E. L. Doctorow takes place in a Dakota mining town called Hard Times. It is a fit name for a place in the middle of nowhere with harsh weather, hard relationships, bad men and lawlessness. One of the most interesting westerns I have ever read because of its' plot. Hard Times is filled with desperation and hopelessness. Sounds like a cookie-cutter western? It's not, but to say more would give it all away.<br/><br/>My rating: 5 stars. This book is classic worthy.</span>

<div id="review_1866481090" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1866481090" />
    <a title="Ned" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21232850-ned"><img alt="Ned" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1526900273p2/21232850.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1866481090?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ned" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ned" href="/user/show/21232850-ned">Ned</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/10595533-welcome-to-hard-times">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1866481090" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16932826253296255069">This was a neat little fable of original sin in the wilderness of territory that was to become South Dakota. Blue, the de facto mayor and conscience of the “town”, meets his nemesis in the form of a human malevolent wrecking crew. The “man” (satan, really) is unleashed on the dry plains and lays to ruin the best efforts of man. This is an origin story of father, mother and child struggle against the mighty force, one with a seething vengeance, one with angry devotion and mixed loved, and the pro</span>
  <span id="freeText16932826253296255069" style="display:none">This was a neat little fable of original sin in the wilderness of territory that was to become South Dakota. Blue, the de facto mayor and conscience of the “town”, meets his nemesis in the form of a human malevolent wrecking crew. The “man” (satan, really) is unleashed on the dry plains and lays to ruin the best efforts of man. This is an origin story of father, mother and child struggle against the mighty force, one with a seething vengeance, one with angry devotion and mixed loved, and the protagonist Blue who holds out hope and gives his all. The years wintering in this bleak landscape, before the town is re-built, is profoundly frightful, yet spring comes and yields its eternal hope for progress. The three know the day of reckoning will come, and it contaminates their relationships, since deep down they know that they are alone and must face their adversary. This memory seethes and unsettles, constantly agitating their minds and eliminating any true peace of mind. The finale, though predictable, shatters all pretense and the chaos of the hordes explodes once the veneer of society is punctured (by the loss of the town’s source of revenue). The town is the world, its people humanity, and the lonely Blue cannot hold the center – forever weakened by his heart’s desire for mankind’s beneficence. Only briefly, at the end, does he taste the sweet yet poisonous fruit of original sin…”and it was thrilling to concentrate my hate” (p. 204)<br /><br />The backdrop will evince Dexter’s “Deadwood”, and the dialogue is true and the colorful characters biblical in the style of McCarthy. This is an entertaining parable where man’s natural tendencies are laid bare and overwhelm the single, heart wrenching (and ultimately feeble) strivings of the one good man whose eyes are always on the buzzards circling in the hot skies above the vast plain stretching out to infinity.<br /><br />Yes, I'll keep reading this author... expecting great things.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16932826253296255069" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2607849846" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2607849846" />
    <a title="Ian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31384949-ian"><img alt="Ian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584889764p2/31384949.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2607849846?book_show_action=true">Dec 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian" href="/user/show/31384949-ian">Ian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780812978223" href="/book/show/1740510.Welcome_to_Hard_Times">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=4-star-lit-fiction">4-star-lit-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=usa">usa</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2607849846" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12733942130501601731">This is the first book I’ve read by E. L. Doctorow, and it was his first, written in 1960. He captures a change to the Zeitgeist, in that this novel is very much a move away from the traditional western. There were aspects that reminded me of the 1973 film “High Plains Drifter”, to the extent I wondered whether the makers of that film had drawn some of their inspiration from “Welcome to Hard Times”. It’s not a perfect comparison though, as the novel has a deeper strain of pessimism. <br/><br/>So, Hard Tim</span>
  <span id="freeText12733942130501601731" style="display:none">This is the first book I’ve read by E. L. Doctorow, and it was his first, written in 1960. He captures a change to the Zeitgeist, in that this novel is very much a move away from the traditional western. There were aspects that reminded me of the 1973 film “High Plains Drifter”, to the extent I wondered whether the makers of that film had drawn some of their inspiration from “Welcome to Hard Times”. It’s not a perfect comparison though, as the novel has a deeper strain of pessimism. <br /><br />So, Hard Times is a one-horse town in the Dakota Territory, and as the novel opens it is visited by a violent psychopath, “The Bad Man from Bodie”, who robs and rapes the inhabitants at will. Most of the townsfolk are too afraid to intervene and those that do are shot dead. Before leaving the killer sets fire to the town’s wooden buildings. Afterwards most of the survivors head off elsewhere, but a motley group stay behind to rebuild the town, led by “Blue”, the unofficial mayor. Blue is a well-meaning but flawed individual. He and his fellow townsfolk are quite successful in bringing the town back, but there is the ever-present thought that the terrifying Man from Bodie may one day return. The story builds to an exciting climax.<br /><br />It’s very much an allegorical novel, but to avoid spoilers I won’t say more.<br /><br />A very impressive first book. A big thank you to my GR Friend Howard, whose review first brought it to my attention.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12733942130501601731" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1270807020" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1270807020" />
    <a title="Craig Childs" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4512914-craig-childs"><img alt="Craig Childs" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586973734p2/4512914.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1270807020?book_show_action=true">May 03, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Craig Childs" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Craig Childs" href="/user/show/4512914-craig-childs">Craig Childs</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/10595533-welcome-to-hard-times">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1270807020" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1597801735902135590">I once believed Larry McMurtry was the first author to write a true anti-Western, a demystification of the American West myth, but it turns out that honor should go to E.L. Doctorow who penned this hard little classic a good twenty-six years before anyone had ever heard of Lonesome Dove. <br/><br/>Welcome to Hard Times is the bleak, almost nihilistic tale of Mayor Will Blue, an aging coward living on the western frontier in the Dakota flatlands. Blue watches his friends murdered and his town burned to the</span>
  <span id="freeText1597801735902135590" style="display:none">I once believed Larry McMurtry was the first author to write a true anti-Western, a demystification of the American West myth, but it turns out that honor should go to E.L. Doctorow who penned this hard little classic a good twenty-six years before anyone had ever heard of Lonesome Dove. <br /><br />Welcome to Hard Times is the bleak, almost nihilistic tale of Mayor Will Blue, an aging coward living on the western frontier in the Dakota flatlands. Blue watches his friends murdered and his town burned to the ground by an unnamed gunslinger, unwilling to put himself in harm’s way to stop it. Afterwards, he must take in the few remaining survivors and then attempt to rebuild the town from scratch, but Blue always fears the return of the Bad Man from Bodie and wonders if he will have the courage to fight back next time.<br /><br />Like the best Westerns, this story tackles hard questions about man’s ability to survive in harsh conditions, what it means to build and live in community, and the consequences of confronting weakness within oneself. You get the feeling that Blue’s goals are doomed to failure from the outset—how can a sad man like this find love, or success, or even friendship in such a harsh landscape?—but you root for him because he tries so hard and his goals are noble. <br /><br />Doctorow is doing for the Western what Cormac McCarthy did for the crime novel in No Country for Old Men. He strips the genre down to its bones and removes the pretense of even the possibility of happy endings. This is not a world where the weak are redeemed. Instead, as one character says:<br /><br />“Truth is, if the drought don’t get you and the blizzards don’t get you, that’s when some devil with liquor in his soul and a gun in his claw will ride you down and clean you out.”<br /><br />In this time and space, even the concept of civilization can be shifting and illusory. Another great passage said this: <br /><br />“Every time someone puts a little capital into this Territory I’m called in by the Governor and sent on my way…. If a man files a claim that yields, there’s a town. If he finds some grass, there’s a town. Does he dig a well? Another town. Does he stop somewhere to ease his bladder, there’s a town. Over this land a thousand times each year towns spring up and it appears I have to charter them all. But to what purpose? The claim pinches out, the grass dies, the well dries up, and everyone will ride off to form up again somewhere else for me to travel. Nothing fixes in this damned country, people blow around at the whiff of the wind. You can’t bring the law to a bunch of rocks, you can’t settle the coyotes, you can’t make a society out of sand.”<br /><br />Unfortunately, the story falls apart in the third act. Like many authors, Doctorow seemed to be trying too hard to make a point rather than tell a story. He allows his thematic message—that civilization is a just false veneer, that all men are violent and anarchic by nature—to overwhelm and overshadow the natural narrative arc. Predictably, Blue’s dreams for his new city fall apart, and the Bad Man from Bodie returns for a final showdown. The climactic situation devolves into almost satirical scenes of rioting, murder, and unrestrained mayhem that just did not ring true to the characters or the story itself.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1597801735902135590" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer15140703620569099283">Doctorow's first novel is a literary western. That's right. It was shelved in Westerns at my library. In truth, it is a philosophical though action packed story set in Dakota Territory during the wild, lawless days when the West was being settled.<br/><br/>The writing is taut and just about perfect. You can see, hear, almost smell the town of Hard Times and the characters leap to life. The "Bad Man from Bodie" rides into town, rapes the whores, then burns down the entire town.<br/><br/>Blue is the default philosoph</span>
  <span id="freeText15140703620569099283" style="display:none">Doctorow's first novel is a literary western. That's right. It was shelved in Westerns at my library. In truth, it is a philosophical though action packed story set in Dakota Territory during the wild, lawless days when the West was being settled.<br /><br />The writing is taut and just about perfect. You can see, hear, almost smell the town of Hard Times and the characters leap to life. The "Bad Man from Bodie" rides into town, rapes the whores, then burns down the entire town.<br /><br />Blue is the default philosophizing mayor. In penance for failing to defend his town from the Bad Man because he was not willing to kill the guy, he attempts to rebuild the town and to create a family by taking in Molly, one of the raped whores, as well as the young son of his best friend who died in the fire. Molly reminded me of Kathy from Steinbeck's East of Eden.<br /><br />But evil has visited the town once and Doctorow creates some serious foreboding and foreshadowing. You know it's coming back. Quite a page-turner for such a philosophical book because the symbolism is embedded in the drama.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15140703620569099283" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_283381715" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/283381715" />
    <a title="Shaun" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7202926-shaun"><img alt="Shaun" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/283381715?book_show_action=true">Feb 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shaun" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shaun" href="/user/show/7202926-shaun">Shaun</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer283381715" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9499203406305317048">Coming across as a mixture of Blood Meridian and Mccabe and Mrs. Miller, this revisionist western examines and undermines the myths of the American west while emphasizing the role violence played in carving out civilization. In a strange way, I was also reminded of J.M. Coetzee's Waiting for the Barbarians; the method of narration was quite similar. Mayor Blue's record-keeping is very reminiscent of the Magistrate's journal-keeping. In both cases, there is a town on a frontier fearing the invasi</span>
  <span id="freeText9499203406305317048" style="display:none">Coming across as a mixture of Blood Meridian and Mccabe and Mrs. Miller, this revisionist western examines and undermines the myths of the American west while emphasizing the role violence played in carving out civilization. In a strange way, I was also reminded of J.M. Coetzee's Waiting for the Barbarians; the method of narration was quite similar. Mayor Blue's record-keeping is very reminiscent of the Magistrate's journal-keeping. In both cases, there is a town on a frontier fearing the invasion of an outside menace. Whereas in Coetzee's novel the threat of the barbarians is largely manufactured by the Empire, the Man from Bodie is a real menace to the town of Hard Times. This hidden jewel in Doctorow's distinguished career left me wondering why this book isn't more acclaimed. I went in expecting it to be a relatively minor piece in the career of the man who wrote Ragtime, The Book of Daniel, and Billy Bathgate, and instead discovered a work that can stand alongside any work of postwar fiction. It is so far ahead of its time that its looking behind you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9499203406305317048" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2978775229" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2978775229" />
    <a title="Phyllis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/586425-phyllis"><img alt="Phyllis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1202885645p2/586425.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2978775229?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Phyllis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Phyllis" href="/user/show/586425-phyllis">Phyllis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780812978223" href="/book/show/1740510.Welcome_to_Hard_Times">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/586425-phyllis?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/586425-phyllis?shelf=authors-my-very-favorite">authors-my-very-favorite</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/586425-phyllis?shelf=2020-reading">2020-reading</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/586425-phyllis?shelf=western">western</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/586425-phyllis?shelf=favorites-of-all-time">favorites-of-all-time</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/586425-phyllis?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/586425-phyllis?shelf=americana">americana</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2978775229" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10953387858866995739">This is the western for all those readers who don't think they like westerns. It has bad guys (& gals), and it has good guys (& gals), but mostly it has regular people who are sometimes and in some ways bad and some good and mostly just living their lives as best they can from day to day. I wasn't alive in the days of the wild, wild west, yet I imagine the reality of that time & place was much closer to that portrayed in this novel than in any movie I have ever seen. (I have a sneaking suspicion</span>
  <span id="freeText10953387858866995739" style="display:none">This is the western for all those readers who don't think they like westerns. It has bad guys (&amp; gals), and it has good guys (&amp; gals), but mostly it has regular people who are sometimes and in some ways bad and some good and mostly just living their lives as best they can from day to day. I wasn't alive in the days of the wild, wild west, yet I imagine the reality of that time &amp; place was much closer to that portrayed in this novel than in any movie I have ever seen. (I have a sneaking suspicion that the makers of the HBO series &amp; movie <i>Deadwood</i> likely read Doctorow's book, published in 1960, and loosely drew the characters, story arc, and plot lines from it. I enjoyed that series a lot, but it doesn't hold a candle to this novel.) <br /><br />The story is told by one man named Blue, nominally the unelected mayor but mostly just the keeper of ledgers of the town of Hard Times, and it unfolds from start to finish over about 18 to 24 months. The beginning is a doozy and not for the faint of heart (lots of rough language, lots of violence) -- if you've never imagined how impossibly lawless and every-man-for-himself the tiny communities of the western territories likely were, Doctorow draws a vivid picture. The middle is a deep character exploration of all those residents who survived &amp; remained and of the new arrivals to the town; what drew them &amp; kept them there; how they understood or failed to understand themselves and each other; and how they faced &amp; were affected by the challenges of each changing season. You'll likely love some of the characters and downright despise others, but you're guaranteed to care about them either way. And hold your horses &amp; pour yourself a stiff drink for the ending, because it will break your heart. Honest: I think I died just a little on the last page.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10953387858866995739" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <a title="Malum" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Malum" href="/user/show/68337677-malum">Malum</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68337677-malum?shelf=western">western</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3501122056" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10804304161982198502">A powerful and bleak novel where there are bad guys and normal folks, but not a hero to be found anywhere.</span>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andy Weston" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andy Weston" href="/user/show/313206-andy-weston">Andy Weston</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/313206-andy-weston?shelf=american-literature">american-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/313206-andy-weston?shelf=western">western</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/313206-andy-weston?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2037237452" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14888486755283332399">Doctorow's story set in the wild Dakota hills is of the American west is not one that tells of heroes and acts of bravery, but rather the reverse. Blue has the de-facto title of Mayor is a small rural town when one day it is shaken by the visit of man intent of evil. With buildings burnt and many of its inhabitants killed, Blue takes on the rebuilding, along with the other survivors, the key characters in the book. <br/><br/><i>"I picture some reader, a gentleman in a stuffed chair with a rug under him and a</i></span>
  <span id="freeText14888486755283332399" style="display:none">Doctorow's story set in the wild Dakota hills is of the American west is not one that tells of heroes and acts of bravery, but rather the reverse. Blue has the de-facto title of Mayor is a small rural town when one day it is shaken by the visit of man intent of evil. With buildings burnt and many of its inhabitants killed, Blue takes on the rebuilding, along with the other survivors, the key characters in the book. <br /><br /><i>"I picture some reader, a gentleman in a stuffed chair with a rug under him and a solid house around him and a whole city of stone streets around the house - a place like New York which Molly talked about one night, with gas lamps on each corner to light ten dark, and polished carriages running behind the horses, and lots of fine manners...Do you think mister, with all that settlement around you that you're freer than me to make your fate? Do you click your tongue at my story?"</i><br /><br />Blue is a very different character to the leads of other books about the American west at this time, an antihero. Molly is recovering from being badly burnt in the town's destruction, but is free with insults to the Mayor in what he should have done. Jimmy is a 12 year old boy whose father was brutally killed. As they and the other survivors piece together a life, the town has a resurgence as gold miners arrive. But Hard Times is not about to earn a change of name or fortune. <br /><br />This is a hugely powerful story, and first published in 1960, it is easy to see why it has become a classic of recent times. It's start and ending are particularly strong. Doctorow describes his characters so wonderfully well that for the duration of your read of this short book you are caught up with their ordeal.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14888486755283332399" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer77046032" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8321037009487000472">I love Westerns and Doctorow, as expected, turns out a great one especially considering this was his first published book. Blue, a hyper responsible, self appointed mayor devotes himself to keeping town records. People naturally turn to him when a mean gunslinger hits town. He fails them, horrible things happen, lots of scared people scatter and desert the town of Hard Times. Blue takes the few remaining people under his wing including the badly burned and terrorized lady of the night and an orp</span>
  <span id="freeText8321037009487000472" style="display:none">I love Westerns and Doctorow, as expected, turns out a great one especially considering this was his first published book. Blue, a hyper responsible, self appointed mayor devotes himself to keeping town records. People naturally turn to him when a mean gunslinger hits town. He fails them, horrible things happen, lots of scared people scatter and desert the town of Hard Times. Blue takes the few remaining people under his wing including the badly burned and terrorized lady of the night and an orphaned boy. He welcomes newcomers and entices them to stay and help him rebuild the town. The problem everyone spends their time waiting for the bad man to return. A black cloud hangs over Hard Times even as it grows again and Blue scribbles about it. If you like Westerns I'd advise against missing this one.</span>

<div id="review_1381623622" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1381623622" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1820008-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1381623622?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/1820008-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1381623622" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3487930810551144404">A tragic scenario though philosophically realistic as an allegory on what a community back then got, and any will again, when there's no organized society/community or, as Thomas Paine would put it, with out the necessary evil of government based on the common good.<br/><br/>Libertarian anarchism brought to life in frontier times (some time in the 19th century, it seems). And it's also where America is headed if we don't reverse course on the extremist Dominionism and Tea Party based exploitation of the 9</span>
  <span id="freeText3487930810551144404" style="display:none">A tragic scenario though philosophically realistic as an allegory on what a community back then got, and any will again, when there's no organized society/community or, as Thomas Paine would put it, with out the necessary evil of government based on the common good.<br /><br />Libertarian anarchism brought to life in frontier times (some time in the 19th century, it seems). And it's also where America is headed if we don't reverse course on the extremist Dominionism and Tea Party based exploitation of the 98 percent by the elites.<br /><br />Genuinely, this seems to be somewhat how life is in failed African states such as Somalia. Survival of the fittest. But the fittest generally don't build their lives around what it takes to build a successful community.<br /><br />An unsuspecting pioneer town with a mayor by default (everybody just thinks Blue's the guy who has it together enough to provide wisdom for building the town). But even though townsfolk expect him to protect them, he chickens out. Enter the Bad Man from Bodie, who literally rapes and pillages and burns the town down, terrorizing those who stuck around.<br /><br />After which, they rebuild, while fearfully anticipating the Bad Man's return someday in the future. And the Bad Man does return.<br /><br />Will they live happily ever after? Well, they didn't get public safety established. They had promise of economic good times, which ended up going up in dust, so to speak.<br /><br />So, what kind of gumption does it take to build a successful community/town/state?<br /><br />So, if you have a friend who likes to read westerns and is trying to figure out whether Libertarian ideals are workable, this might be a good book for him to explore as food for thought.<br /><br />I'm not enough of a reader that I'm ready to give a five star rating to a book that may not have quite as happy of an ending I'd like. But it was still very good and thought provoking.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3487930810551144404" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evan" href="/user/show/60356208-evan">Evan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2983975705" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4291261358046799889">Reminiscent of Hemingway in it's unadorned style. But maybe better for those soul-stirring elegies, like this one at the very end:<br/><br/>"Nothing is ever buried, the earth rolls in it's tracks, it never goes anywhere, it never changes, only the hope changes like morning and night, only the expectations rise and set. Why does there have to be promise before destruction?"<br/><br/>Or this one a few pages earlier:<br/><br/>"...the only thing growing was trouble; and it made me shudder to think whatever perfection was, like t</span>
  <span id="freeText4291261358046799889" style="display:none">Reminiscent of Hemingway in it's unadorned style. But maybe better for those soul-stirring elegies, like this one at the very end:<br /><br />"Nothing is ever buried, the earth rolls in it's tracks, it never goes anywhere, it never changes, only the hope changes like morning and night, only the expectations rise and set. Why does there have to be promise before destruction?"<br /><br />Or this one a few pages earlier:<br /><br />"...the only thing growing was trouble; and it made me shudder to think whatever perfection was, like the perfection I had with Molly, it was maybe past, silently come and gone, a moment long, just the instant in the shadow of one day, and any fool who was still waiting for it, like he dreamed, didn't know what life is."<br /><br />Side note: I read this to accompany Stephen King's "The Shining." "Welcome to Hard Times" is conspicuously referenced in King's novel (Jack Torrance, by no coincidence, entertains himself with Doctorow's debut novel while sitting in his truck outside The Overlook). Clearly the book had a significant influence on King during this period. Both stories detail families led by sentimental and overly optimistic father figures whose relentless pursuit of the elusive "American Dream" is symbolized by a hasty trek Westward, and which in both cases results in self-destruction.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4291261358046799889" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1178453940" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Lorentz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Lorentz" href="/user/show/39511999-paul-lorentz">Paul Lorentz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39511999-paul-lorentz?shelf=fiction-20th-century-american">fiction-20th-century-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1178453940" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15480423061408724892">One of my favorites, this is maybe the fifth or sixth time I've read it, the first since visiting South Dakota a few years ago. Opening with the sudden and senseless, horrifically violent destruction of a barely-hanging-on town in the Dakota Territories, it is a recounting of events as recorded in three ledgers by the town's "Mayor", a glorified bookkeeper named Blue. By dumb luck, willfully blind optimism, and a persuasive way with words, Blue and the remaining survivors (a brutally victimized </span>
  <span id="freeText15480423061408724892" style="display:none">One of my favorites, this is maybe the fifth or sixth time I've read it, the first since visiting South Dakota a few years ago. Opening with the sudden and senseless, horrifically violent destruction of a barely-hanging-on town in the Dakota Territories, it is a recounting of events as recorded in three ledgers by the town's "Mayor", a glorified bookkeeper named Blue. By dumb luck, willfully blind optimism, and a persuasive way with words, Blue and the remaining survivors (a brutally victimized and understandably spiteful barmaid, an orphaned teenager, and a deaf-and-dumb Indian doctor) and a few enterprising (however unlucky) souls who happen upon the place struggle to rebuild it into a proper town, better than before, fit to show up on a map. Once an aspiring New England lawyer, Blue started wandering out west following the death of his wife and young child. Approaching 50 years old, Blue has a "grass-isn't-greener-anywhere-ever" outlook but he pursues the project of his Little Town on the Prairie with a maddening sticktoitiveness. I always know it can't end well, and maybe it should worry me how much I relate to Blue and his relentless (however futile and tragic) optimism, but I always love going back to Hard Times. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15480423061408724892" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1356573469" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9823532036715397695">This is Doctorow’s first book written in 1960. I understand a movie was made from the book staring Henry Fonda. The book’s genre is classified as a serious western.<br/><br/> “Welcome to Hard Times” is about average people who fail to organize and stand as one against evil and never recover from the devastating results of their failure to act. The story also has many subplots for a book that is only 224 pages or 6 hours audio.<br/><br/>Doctorow has the traditional characters of a typical western, a bullying evil vi</span>
  <span id="freeText9823532036715397695" style="display:none">This is Doctorow’s first book written in 1960. I understand a movie was made from the book staring Henry Fonda. The book’s genre is classified as a serious western.<br /><br /> “Welcome to Hard Times” is about average people who fail to organize and stand as one against evil and never recover from the devastating results of their failure to act. The story also has many subplots for a book that is only 224 pages or 6 hours audio.<br /><br />Doctorow has the traditional characters of a typical western, a bullying evil villain, the noble prostitute and the unwilling hero. But the author provides the reader with a big twist in that the hero fails to act. I was reminded of one of my favorite movies “High Noon” but in Doctorow’s story there is no Gary Cooper or John Wayne to do the job.<br /><br />The book provides an important lesson. The book is well written and moves at a fast pace. It is easy to read for a story with such an important topic. I read this as an audiobook downloaded from Audible. John Rubinstein did a great job narrating the story.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9823532036715397695" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText773333520136367910" style="display:none">"Nothing is ever buried, the earth rolls over in its tracks, it never goes anywhere, it never changes, only the hope changes like morning and night, only the expectations rise and set. Why does there have to be a promise before destruction?"<br /><br />This simple novel, set in the Dakota flats well before statehood, is written simply and directly and somehow contains a few sharp insights and phrases to be found echoing through all of Tragedy. To be sure, these thoughts are not original. There is no originality to the tragic. However, Doctorow has put his American (screenwriter, at the time, though this was not originally a screenplay it was eventually turned into a -bad- film) spin onto the territory. <br /><br />I read it in a matter of hours and though I guess it has an inevitability, felt caught up and this is entirely due to the writing, the gentle way he manages to unfold his take on the persistent (inspiring? maddening? I guess that depends on your philosophy) folly of humans. </span>

I liked the way Doctorow explored what happens when the helpless are prayed upon by pure irrational evil. It is not a pretty sight. Although I read the story in one weekend I would not call it a quick light read. The author's most famous bo</span>
  <span id="freeText7283321110975881465" style="display:none">I never read a western quite like this. Well read and disturbing it is quite the book. I liked the way Doctorow explored what happens when the helpless are prayed upon by pure irrational evil. It is not a pretty sight. Although I read the story in one weekend I would not call it a quick light read. The author's most famous book is "The March" and it has made its way on my to be read list.</span>

It was too terrible to be a favorite. Yet it definitely was a powerful novel. It was almost like the devil himself had bestowed his powers upon this novel. Unsettling is the best adjective I can summon for describing it. And it was just too much for a first novel.<br/></span>

<div id="review_3524156459" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Tasha Robinson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10244904-tasha-robinson"><img alt="Tasha Robinson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420923703p2/10244904.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3524156459?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tasha Robinson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tasha Robinson" href="/user/show/10244904-tasha-robinson">Tasha Robinson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3524156459" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16720929986736533333">I've never read Doctorow, but I picked this one up because Stephen King's latest book heavily praises Doctorow and JD MacDonald — and reading this book, I feel like I understand more about where King's voice comes from. This is more of a Western than a horror story, but much like King's work, it bleeds across genres, and there certainly is a strong horror element in the descriptions of death and destruction in a small town, and the privation and madness that follows, and the heavy sense of inevi</span>
  <span id="freeText16720929986736533333" style="display:none">I've never read Doctorow, but I picked this one up because Stephen King's latest book heavily praises Doctorow and JD MacDonald — and reading this book, I feel like I understand more about where King's voice comes from. This is more of a Western than a horror story, but much like King's work, it bleeds across genres, and there certainly is a strong horror element in the descriptions of death and destruction in a small town, and the privation and madness that follows, and the heavy sense of inevitability after that. This is a lean, simple book, but it's descriptive and "voice-y" for all that, with a strong sense of place and character. Reminded me of Daniel Woodrell a fair bit.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16720929986736533333" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3352755606" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9750267817472759438">I agree with Stephen King that this is one of the best westerns ever written. For it to be Doctorow's first novel at the age of 29 is even more remarkable. I have read many of his books but did not even know he had written a western. Strongly recommend this author and this book.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1936096965" />
    <a title="Pamela" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9157790-pamela"><img alt="Pamela" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1335752056p2/9157790.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1936096965?book_show_action=true">Mar 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pamela" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pamela" href="/user/show/9157790-pamela">Pamela</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1936096965" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15197005295412755791">OK - we read this a book club selection.<br/><br/>I have mixed feelings about it - it is well written: I think the choice and use of language is very good.<br/>it is - evocative? atmospheric? - the author does a good job of evoking a mood.<br/>characters - reasonably well done - it is written from the POV of one of the characters so we only see what he thinks about other people's acts and attitudes and we have no real information about what THEY might think of him, or anything else.<br/><br/>That said: I really didn't like i</span>
  <span id="freeText15197005295412755791" style="display:none">OK - we read this a book club selection.<br /><br />I have mixed feelings about it - it is well written: I think the choice and use of language is very good.<br />it is - evocative? atmospheric? - the author does a good job of evoking a mood.<br />characters - reasonably well done - it is written from the POV of one of the characters so we only see what he thinks about other people's acts and attitudes and we have no real information about what THEY might think of him, or anything else.<br /><br />That said: I really didn't like it very well. I suppose it is a fair depiction of a certain kind of iconic western story. Personally, I prefer the ones that have least ONE redeeming, admirable (even if flawed) character.<br /><br />I think this is the kind of book you'd like if you enjoyed Hamlet, where the main character dithers through the entire story, does all the wrong things at the wrong times (or the right things at the wrong times) and ends up getting everybody killed in the end, without ever figuring out why his own character made it inevitable.<br /><br />I have never liked Hamlet much. He was too busy being noble, and forgot to be good.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15197005295412755791" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('VGbLllYhp5WaZZaMw77qZ86+hVGwSCXxORVKJz1zt0soQieR0lGE21ARW0AULpdU9H8nKh9fSDG+pz+jVr7IzQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

<script>

In classic Western style Larry McMurtry illustrates the timeless conflict between the modernity and the Old West through the eyes of Texas cattlemen.Horseman, Pass By tells the story of Homer Bannon, an old-time cattleman who epitomizes the frontier values of honesty and decency, and Hud, his unscrupulous stepson. Caught in the middle is the narrator, Homer\'s young grandson Lonnie, who is as much drawn to his grandfather’s strength of character as he is to Hud\'s hedonism and materialism. When first published in 1961, Horseman, Pass By caused a sensation in Texas literary circles for its stark, realistic portrayal of the struggles of a changing West in the years following World War II. Never before had a writer managed to encapsulate its environment with such unsentimental realism. Today, memorable characters, powerful themes, and illuminating detail make Horseman, Pass By vintage McMurtry.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"15326447619986877653\" href=\"#\"
